  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8c64754f-4bcc-4ef9-9eaf-2e46ab7429e0'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I could not have been added by him in 1999 because he was dead. <br/><br/>The Del Rey Impact paperback edition says the book was copyrighted 1963 by Tevis and r<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">â€¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I could not have been added by him in 1999 because he was dead. <br /><br />The Del Rey Impact paperback edition says the book was copyrighted 1963 by Tevis and renewed 1991 by his widow. <br /><br />So either it was updated by the editors for the 1991 copyright, or the copyright notice is incorrect. <br /><br />Glad I found this question here. It bugged the hell out of me when I read that paragraph. <br /><br />Can anyone with an earlier edition respond with what the original paragraph said?<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Looked at my 1963 edition, The "Gold Medal Original" Published by Frederick Muller Limited London. By arrangement with Fawcett Publications Inc. Chapt<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">â€¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Looked at my 1963 edition, The "Gold Medal Original" Published by Frederick Muller Limited London. By arrangement with Fawcett Publications Inc. Chapter 8 last paragraph page 121 had the following " ...1976 is an election year. The President is already campaigning for a second term ... did you know that the President uses us, in the CIA, to spy on the other party? - that the Republicans are going to turn this whole business into something like the Dreyfus case" No mention of Watergate in the original.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4570146409721077672"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604775936i/30348626._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>This story is more psychological than science fiction. The main character, Thomas Jerome Newton, is an extraterrestrial from a planet called Anthea. He came to Earth in hopes of designing, manufacturing, and launching several spaceships to return to his home planet. His home planet has become a desert wasteland ravaged by nuclear war. He was chosen by his people to venture to Earth and undertake the rescue mission. <br/><br/>Newton uses the advanced technology from his home planet to earn millions and fin</span>
  <span id="freeText4570146409721077672" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604775936i/30348626._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>This story is more psychological than science fiction. The main character, Thomas Jerome Newton, is an extraterrestrial from a planet called Anthea. He came to Earth in hopes of designing, manufacturing, and launching several spaceships to return to his home planet. His home planet has become a desert wasteland ravaged by nuclear war. He was chosen by his people to venture to Earth and undertake the rescue mission. <br><br>Newton uses the advanced technology from his home planet to earn millions and finance the manufacturing of the spacecraft but he falls short. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604775935i/30348625._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>'He stared at himself in the mirror for a long time crying. Then he spoke aloud, to himself, in English. "Who are you?", he said. "And where do you belong?" <br>'And on the couch, Thomas Jerome Newton now lay drinking, his gin bottle open, his eyes glazed and staring at the ceiling in anguish...' pg. 98<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604776085i/30348634._SX540_.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The longer he spends on Earth, he slowly changes and becomes more human-like. He enters a downward spiral as he develops a dependence on alcohol, starts to experience major depressive symptoms like loneliness, emotional and social alienation, demotivation, self-doubt, and self-pity. He eventually becomes stranded on Earth doomed a failure. <br><br>This was my first Walter Tevis novel and I enjoyed it. The readability and his ability to inject human emotion into the story was great. I would recommend this to anyone looking for something original and entertaining. Thanks!</span>

<div id="review_323034580" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/323034580" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/323034580?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=pre-80s-sf">pre-80s-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer323034580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1867232484536449829"><i>â€œThe man was very odd. Tall, thin, with white hair and a fine, delicate bone structure. He had smooth skin and a boyish face â€“ but the eyes were very strange, as though they were weak, over-sensitive, yet with a look that was old and wise and tired.â€</i><br/><br/>In short, he looks a lot like this:<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607701._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='259' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Ground control to Major Thomas?<br/><br/>The eponymous Man Who Fell to Earth is one Thomas Jerome Newton, a rather commonplace name, not alienesque like Xarx or something along that line. Of course, his real name is very unli</span>
  <span id="freeText1867232484536449829" style="display:none"><i>â€œThe man was very odd. Tall, thin, with white hair and a fine, delicate bone structure. He had smooth skin and a boyish face â€“ but the eyes were very strange, as though they were weak, over-sensitive, yet with a look that was old and wise and tired.â€</i><br><br>In short, he looks a lot like this:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607701._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="259" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Ground control to Major Thomas?<br><br>The eponymous Man Who Fell to Earth is one Thomas Jerome Newton, a rather commonplace name, not alienesque like Xarx or something along that line. Of course, his real name is very unlikely to be Thomas Jerome Newton; he is from a planet he calls â€œAnthea*â€, but if he has an Anthean name it is never mentioned. He has come to Earth on a solo super-secret mission, so secret that he destroys the single seat spaceship he came in on. Using his knowledge of hyper-advanced science he soon builds a business empire selling amazing new products like â€“ uh â€“ a high-speed film that develops itself, a new kind of TV, stereo systems, and various gadgets. Things were going according to plan until the pesky, primitive humans become his friends. <s>Thrilling adventure</s> Disillusionment, loss of identity and heartbreak ensue.<br><br><i>The Man Who Fell to Earth</i> was first published in 1963, the story is set in 1985 and spans five years or so. This being the case some of the alien science is rather anachronistic in term of our real world science today; for example super high-quality films instead of digital photography. I suspect <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7779.Arthur_C__Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener"> Arthur C. Clarke</a> would have fared better with the imaginary gadgetry but this novel is clearly not about the science. The central theme seems to be loneliness and its impact on your goals. There are also observations about humanityâ€™s lack of appreciation for the riches of our planet, the futility of war, and some satire of government bureaucratic incompetence. <br><br>Due to Earthâ€™s higher gravity, Newton is physically weak and frail, but he also seems unlikely to have the strength of character to set up a business empire. While on a business trip he meets a hard drinking hillbilly girl called Betty Jo helps him out when he has an accident. They do not become romantically involved but he hires her as his personally assistant, he picks up her taste for gin and they soon go on benders together. After a few years on Earth with no way to communicate with his home planet Newton begins to feel lonely and miserable, he also feels that he has gone native in that he now thinks more like a human than an Anthean. The interrelationship between Newton and several characters he comes to call friends is quite sweet and touching. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607704.jpg" width="400" height="172" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Thereâ€™s a starmanâ€¦<br><br>A feeling of melancholy pervades throughout the book. Newton is not a happy alien even when things are going well for him, and when they go south the man who fell to earth probably wishes he had fallen somewhere else. Newton is a nuanced, sympathetic character. However, if you take out the â€œsymâ€ part of sympathetic he is also that. It is hard to believe the Antheans chose him for this mission for his stamina.<br><br><i>The Man Who Fell to Earth</i> is a very good read, I cannot find any serious flaw with it. However, I feel that the book ends too abruptly and somewhat inconclusively so I am holding back my often overly lenient five stars rating. I can recommend without hesitation if you like thoughtful, contemplative and somewhat philosophical sci-fi.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Notes:</b><br>* Anthea is implied to be Mars, but by 1963 scientists already suspect that there is no life on Mars, so Tevis avoids using the name.<br><br>â€¢ Decades ago I saw the <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0074851/" rel="nofollow noopener">1976 film adaptation </a>, starring David Bowie, I thought it was weird and could not make heads or tails of it. The source material here is a breeze to read.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607703.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>â€¢ There is a scene where Newton is interrogated by government agents, this is a notable scene because it is extremely well written and vivid. <br><br>â€¢ Don't read the goddam intro by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/108281.Ken_MacLeod" title="Ken MacLeod" rel="nofollow noopener"> Ken MacLeod </a> before reading the novel because it's riddled with spoilers. Not cool Mr. MacLeod! <br><br>â€¢ Walter Tevisâ€™ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1881236240" rel="nofollow noopener">Mockingbird</a> is fabulous, donâ€™t miss it.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493371051i/22607702._SX540_.png" width="100" height="75" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Quotes:</b><br>â€œThere are times when you seem, to us, like apes loose in a museum, carrying knives, slashing the canvases, breaking the statuary with hammers.â€<br><br><i>â€œHow human he had become, to rationalize that way! He blamed her for his going native and becoming obsessed with vague guilts and vaguer doubts. She had taught him to drink gin; and she had shown him an aspect of strong and comfortable and hedonistic and unthinking humanity that his fifteen years of studying television had left him unaware of.â€<br><br>â€œHe felt like a man who has been surrounded by reasonably amiable, silly, and fairly intelligent animals, and has gradually discovered that their concepts and relationships are more complex than his training could have led him to suspect. Such a man might discover that, in one or more of the many aspects of weighing and judging that are available to a high intelligence, the animals who surround him and who foul their own lairs and eat their own filth might be happier and wiser than he.â€<br><br>â€œIt dismays us greatly to see what you are about to do with such a beautiful, fertile world. We destroyed ours a long time ago, but we had so much less to begin with than you have here.â€™ His voice now seemed agitated, his manner more intense. â€˜Do you realize that you will not only wreck your civilization, such as it is, and kill most of your people; but that you will also poison the fish in your rivers, the squirrels in your trees, the flocks of birds, the soil, the water?â€</i></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137088227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14187622666965583427">I had nearly forgotten why people start reading in the first place: the joy of an honest story. I'm so used to the writer as the essential protagonist, the writing as his conflict, and whether or not I want to throw away his book as his comedic or tragic end. But this just unfolds cleanly, without seeming consciously written at all. Never an "ohhh that was beautiful" and very rarely a distracting wince. I got deeply engaged without any self-discipline at all. <br/>It's lightening-quick and so satisfy</span>
  <span id="freeText14187622666965583427" style="display:none">I had nearly forgotten why people start reading in the first place: the joy of an honest story. I'm so used to the writer as the essential protagonist, the writing as his conflict, and whether or not I want to throw away his book as his comedic or tragic end. But this just unfolds cleanly, without seeming consciously written at all. Never an "ohhh that was beautiful" and very rarely a distracting wince. I got deeply engaged without any self-discipline at all. <br />It's lightening-quick and so satisfying that I had to, for the first time, linger in a subway station to finish a book because I didn't want to wait until I got home. (And that precise location, my friends, was the enigmatic, Escher-like layer of the W 4th St. station, between the blue and the orange floors, quite possibly the smelliest place out there, just to attest to the refreshment of the reading experience.)<br />I have the DVD and cannot wait to watch it - maybe even tonight.<br />Why only 4 stars then? Because I'm still moonlighting as a snob. And, as such, switching back to Gravity's Rainbow and its two essential companions. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14187622666965583427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/640667-sarah?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14602994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12216140327471251272">Brilliant. This a deceptively simple story, told in simple, uncomplicated prose, but with unexpected depth and relevance. It might come off as slightly trite now, as with most mid-20th century fiction set in "the near future" (the late 1980s, of all things!), but I'm sure in 1963 it was truly a sign of the times. What I'm sure hasn't lost its charge over the years is the tint of sadness, of individualized despair, that permeates the book and ultimately embitters the characters. No one escapes th</span>
  <span id="freeText12216140327471251272" style="display:none">Brilliant. This a deceptively simple story, told in simple, uncomplicated prose, but with unexpected depth and relevance. It might come off as slightly trite now, as with most mid-20th century fiction set in "the near future" (the late 1980s, of all things!), but I'm sure in 1963 it was truly a sign of the times. What I'm sure hasn't lost its charge over the years is the tint of sadness, of individualized despair, that permeates the book and ultimately embitters the characters. No one escapes their self-destructive fears - not the American government, not the curious scientist, and most especially not the titular visitor who comes to save his world but can't even save himself. The film version, starring David Bowie, is far more surreal and symbolically charged (and, as with any Nicholas Roeg film, obsessed with sexuality), but the plot is almost completely the same, and anyone who enjoys one version of the tale should enjoy the other. Definitely worth seeking out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12216140327471251272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_77575686" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77575686" />
    <a title="Maureen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/890233-maureen"><img alt="Maureen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351280684p2/890233.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/77575686?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maureen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maureen" href="/user/show/890233-maureen">Maureen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/890233-maureen?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/890233-maureen?shelf=somebody-awesome-gave-this-to-me">somebody-awesome-gave-this-to-me</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77575686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer820883815613072221">no ray guns are fired or space battles waged in this poignant novel. there is a spaceship yes, but it is incapacitated after it deposits its passenger on earth. the passenger is an alien from a dying planet named anthea and he's looking for an escape - a place for the remnants of his people. his name on earth will be t.j. newton (sometimes called tommy) and this novel is his story, of how our world affects him, physically and emotionally, as he tries to achieve his mission.<br/><br/>there's not much more </span>
  <span id="freeText820883815613072221" style="display:none">no ray guns are fired or space battles waged in this poignant novel. there is a spaceship yes, but it is incapacitated after it deposits its passenger on earth. the passenger is an alien from a dying planet named anthea and he's looking for an escape - a place for the remnants of his people. his name on earth will be t.j. newton (sometimes called tommy) and this novel is his story, of how our world affects him, physically and emotionally, as he tries to achieve his mission.<br /><br />there's not much more of a plot. this is a quiet novel, engaged with the dissolution of a human being not of earth by its influence, by his alienation. he is entirely outside and his loneliness and despair at the earth and the oblivion of its people is deeply felt. despite the fact that the novel is in some ways dated, it is also a wise indictment, a rumination on the world that we live in, how we choose to live in it, and the people that take it for granted. it seems only to get smaller and quieter toward the end... and then it winks out.<br /><br />sad. it made me cry. and it was and made me really thoughtful. and apparently tevis said, it's fairly autobiographical.<br /><br />i felt your pain, newton/tevis. i hope it made it easier to share the load.<br /><br />* i have never seen the film. i can see why bowie was chosen to portray newton. i read that there's more sex in the film and it veers slightly away from the book in that regard. i can't imagine the film would be able to replicate the immersion in the disoriented and sad psyche of newton as he starts to realize he's not equipped to deal with being on earth; of being with him as he starts to crumble. <br /><br />** i'd wanted to see the film before i realized there was a book because of its influence on the philip k. dick novel, VALIS. in fact there is a film based on the man who fell to earth in that book. i can perfectly understand how a film with such a premise would impress upon the mind of pkd -- its themes refract back from his own work. he was clearly taken by bowie in the role but a quick internet search doesn't show me any evidence that he actually ever picked up tevis' book in consequence of seeing the film but i can only think he would have approved of it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="820883815613072221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1874542118288165721"><b>SF Masterworks (2010- series) #81</b>: A story with multiple points of view of an alien invasion, by one alien, Thomas Jerome Newton. What does he want, why does he need to amass huge sums of money? What happened when Newton met alcohol? And what happens to alien surrounded by billions of children, the human race? A deceptively simple, action-less and short story that uses science fiction to tell a very human story, that of alcoholism, loneliness and mankind's knowing walk to global self-destruction</span>
  <span id="freeText1874542118288165721" style="display:none"><b>SF Masterworks (2010- series) #81</b>: A story with multiple points of view of an alien invasion, by one alien, Thomas Jerome Newton. What does he want, why does he need to amass huge sums of money? What happened when Newton met alcohol? And what happens to alien surrounded by billions of children, the human race? A deceptively simple, action-less and short story that uses science fiction to tell a very human story, that of alcoholism, loneliness and mankind's knowing walk to global self-destruction.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1593960451ra/29765842.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>From the writer of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/323170.The_Hustler" title="The Hustler by Walter Tevis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hustler</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26450743.The_Color_of_Money" title="The Color of Money by Walter Tevis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Color of Money</a>, it came as a surprise to me, that he also wrote such notable science-fiction. A book written during the Cold War and as lot of art at the time is heavily influenced by it. The story also has aged really well, so it comes as no surprise that CBS are looking to make a TV show of it! There's not much to fault the story, but does feel like there's a bigger story going that we don't get to see because of the limited point of views of Newton and a scientist, Bryce that we're given. 7 out of 12/<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1593960451ra/29765841.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

  <span id="freeText884794311762970889" style="display:none">Only came to this book through a winding road of Valis by PKD in his description of David Bowie in the novel. That being said, I did like the book quite a bit, being an outsider type of novel with a lot to say about those earthling aliens. Fun read and well done, well worth being a classic, although perhaps it doesn't date as well as other novels. No heavy science, just a sociological discussion. </span>

  <span id="freeText12420755825779510972" style="display:none">The literary equivilant of a flower growing through a block of cement. The characterization is clumsy, obvious and in the case of the gin soaked country woman straight out of "Lady For A Day" borders on self parody. The social commentary is sometimes bizarre such as when our alien friend ruminates on the pros and cons of The Welfare State and sometimes obvious with it's JESUS WAS TEH ALIEN subtext. <div id="review_3080524046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3080524046" />
    <a title="Philip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7413069-philip"><img alt="Philip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583291726p2/7413069.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3080524046?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip" href="/user/show/7413069-philip">Philip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345490100" href="/book/show/93226.The_Man_Who_Fell_To_Earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=filmed">filmed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3080524046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10409286854006620658">Bizarre, trippy mid-60s book that was made into a bizarre, trippy mid-70s movie. I'm reluctant to even call this a work of science fiction; it's more a work of psychological fiction told via a sci-fi setting. In some ways, it also reminds me of Cold War spy fiction, like the TV show "The Americans" - focusing not so much on action as what happens to foreigners/aliens who spend years learning how to pass as humans/Americans, but then can't quite pull it off, or reconcile their mission with their </span>
  <span id="freeText10409286854006620658" style="display:none">Bizarre, trippy mid-60s book that was made into a bizarre, trippy mid-70s movie. I'm reluctant to even call this a work of science fiction; it's more a work of psychological fiction told via a sci-fi setting. In some ways, it also reminds me of Cold War spy fiction, like the TV show "The Americans" - focusing not so much on action as what happens to foreigners/aliens who spend years learning how to pass as humans/Americans, but then can't quite pull it off, or reconcile their mission with their growing connection to the country/planet they've come to mess with. Anyway...an interesting if slim story, both in length and plot.<br><br>When I saw this movie back in the 70s, I was like, "what? David Bowie acting?" But having now read the book, I can't imagine anyone else playing Thomas Newton- although as Tevis describes him, he's definitely less Ziggy Stardust and more Thin White Duke.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576039937i/28592897.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Not sure who I could or would actually recommend this to, but I enjoyed it. Also like the fact that while it was written in 1963, Tevis has the head of the CIA saying "did you know that Watergate changed <i>nothing?</i>" I know a lot of folks think the author went back and tweaked his book after the movie came out...but I like to think that Tevis himself was a time-traveling Anthean, who was simply giving us a glimpse of what was to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10409286854006620658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2111609495" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2111609495" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13985162-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594126645p2/13985162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2111609495?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/13985162-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2111609495" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9849705815581329769">A true alien visitation, classic Sci-fi tale from 1963 that still reads as if it was written yesterday, well ...almost!<br/><br/>An alien lands on Earth and tries to improve the 'life chances' for his own civilisation and those of both the human race and of planet Earth itself.<br/><br/>Genuinely absorbing, fascinating and intriguing, from start to finish. <br/>Absolutely loved everything about it, never a dull moment.<br/><br/>Found the ending to be so profound, touching and moving - very clever and extremely thought provoking.<br/><br/>F</span>
  <span id="freeText9849705815581329769" style="display:none">A true alien visitation, classic Sci-fi tale from 1963 that still reads as if it was written yesterday, well ...almost!<br /><br />An alien lands on Earth and tries to improve the 'life chances' for his own civilisation and those of both the human race and of planet Earth itself.<br /><br />Genuinely absorbing, fascinating and intriguing, from start to finish. <br />Absolutely loved everything about it, never a dull moment.<br /><br />Found the ending to be so profound, touching and moving - very clever and extremely thought provoking.<br /><br />Film version - David Bowie as 'Tommy' Newton - what typecasting, pure genius - absolutely perfect and so, so, so believable!<br /><br />Anthera - doesn't seem that bad, compared to our own sad and unique 'world of worry' today.<br />Might book my holidays there next year...just to get away from it all.<br /><br />A rich and wonderful classic, not-to-be missed - enjoy! Rating: 5 bright and beautiful extra-terrestrial Antherean stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9849705815581329769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_433411595" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433411595" />
    <a title="Bandit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit"><img alt="Bandit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289488158p2/4528663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/433411595?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bandit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bandit" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit">Bandit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer433411595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7636665354875728949">I've discovered this book completely by chance (having heard of the film but never seen it) and absolutely fell in love with it. Often the book or a movie are being referred to as classic and it has to do more with their age than their contents, this book however is a fine example of a real scifi classic, one that teaches us something about ourselves through a different perspective. If there were no dates in the book at all (dates that might have seemed like a future back when the novel was writ</span>
  <span id="freeText7636665354875728949" style="display:none">I've discovered this book completely by chance (having heard of the film but never seen it) and absolutely fell in love with it. Often the book or a movie are being referred to as classic and it has to do more with their age than their contents, this book however is a fine example of a real scifi classic, one that teaches us something about ourselves through a different perspective. If there were no dates in the book at all (dates that might have seemed like a future back when the novel was written, but are now hopelessly dated), this would have been an incredibly timeless classic, it sort of is anyway. The subject matter is timeless...every man is an island, so may be every alien as well, for this book deals with the basic human condition of loneliness as well or better than any other book of any genre. The things that make us human, the things that isolate us, that define us, our purpose and how it can be obscured and distorted by life's many hurdles, social conditioning, ability to persevere in the face of indifference, not being understood or seen for who you are, identity...Walter Tevis explores these and many other important integral questions with his clever story of a very humane visitor coming to our world. Not every visitor fits in as well as Superman, just like not every human can be a shiny success story. This book makes the reader think, feel and emote just like any good story should. Absolutely excellent. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7636665354875728949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9469700873871773551" style="display:none">Picked this one up somewhere in Edison Park, Chicago, my home in Park Ridge being right across Canfield/Ozanam from the city limits. Its protagonist's good intentions towards us, the extremity under which his own species had found itself and his great loneliness were all very moving. When, years later, the David Bowie film adaptation came out, I went to see it in Manhattan. Although not as powerful as the book, it wasn't an entirely bad effort. <div id="review_2284538839" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer8094532228606750407">Author Walter Tevis said of his book â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€, it was the most autobiographical of all his books. Then you read it, and with a bit of biographical information it makes sense.<br/><br/>â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€ is the story of Thomas Newton, an alien who appears in Kentucky, he has a pocketful of gold rings and a mind full of inventions th</span>
  <span id="freeText8094532228606750407" style="display:none">Author Walter Tevis said of his book â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€, it was the most autobiographical of all his books. At first that seems totally absurd, how is a novel about an alien who comes to the earth with the mission of saving his own planet autobiographical? Then you read it, and with a bit of biographical information it makes sense.<br /><br />â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€ is the story of Thomas Newton, an alien who appears in Kentucky, he has a pocketful of gold rings and a mind full of inventions that heâ€™s brought to earth to make millions of dollars to build a spaceship to bring the rest of his species from their dying planet. There is a sub-plot of scientist Nathan Bryce suspecting Newton is an alien and his efforts to discover if he is from another planet or Connecticut. <br /><br />For such a short book â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€ is filled with lots of themes that different readers are sure to pick up on. Alienation is the most obvious and this was picked up by readers in the late 60â€™s and as visualized by Nicholas Roeg in his 1976 film based on the book. But there are also ideas about environmental issues, the danger of knowledge and its misuse to destroy instead of creating, and finally, the story of ruined genius, a man who has failed in his mission on earth, a man who because he gives in to temptation and doesnâ€™t attain the promise of his genius, which is probably why Tevis felt this work as autobiographical.<br /><br />Like â€œThe Hustlerâ€ â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€ became a classic (though not as quickly as â€œThe Hustlerâ€). Though the style and tone of â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€ are quieter, more subdued than â€œThe Hustlerâ€ they fit the tone and demeanor of Thomas Newton, and I found myself highlighting a great many passages that are exquisite examples of Tevisâ€™ writing, something that only the best writing brings me to do.<br /><br />â€œThe Man Who Fell to Earthâ€ due to the popular culture of movies is a story we think we know, but itâ€™s in the reading Tevisâ€™ words that we find the nuances of Tevisâ€™ art, and why these books have become classics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8094532228606750407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10300759749531453561">On the outside, The Man Who Fell to Earth is about Thomas Jerome Newton, an alien from the planet Anthea, who has come to Earth seeking salvation for the last of his people but it's really about being alone inside your own skin and struggling to feel connected to anyone or anything before you die.<br/><br/>Walter Tevis takes a subject that has been overdone, ooh a spaceship and an alien EXCITING!, and makes his own unique brand of sociopolitical science fiction. The lyrical descriptions mesh well with the</span>
  <span id="freeText10300759749531453561" style="display:none">On the outside, The Man Who Fell to Earth is about Thomas Jerome Newton, an alien from the planet Anthea, who has come to Earth seeking salvation for the last of his people but it's really about being alone inside your own skin and struggling to feel connected to anyone or anything before you die.<br /><br />Walter Tevis takes a subject that has been overdone, ooh a spaceship and an alien EXCITING!, and makes his own unique brand of sociopolitical science fiction. The lyrical descriptions mesh well with the no nonsense dialogue to create a colorful yet direct backdrop to the storyline. While it does edge toward preachiness at times, I feel it was meant more as a warning than a sermon.<br /><br />Written in 1963 it gave us a grim look at a possible future. Reading it in 2010, it is a haunting portrayal of alienation both social and literal that is all too realistic.<br /><br />I'd recommend this to people who enjoy a book with a deeper meaning.</span>

<div id="review_675016149" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/675016149" />
    <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi"><img alt="Lorenzo Berardi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417557095p2/183865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/675016149?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorenzo Berardi" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi">Lorenzo Berardi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141190372" href="/book/show/6914970-the-man-who-fell-to-earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=a-my-english-library">a-my-english-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer675016149" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10575755042692948666"><br/>As a non native English speaker, I discovered the adjective 'poignant' only six years ago thanks to a Canadian friend (thanks, Vicky). She chose it to comment a photo I took involving a bowler hat hanging from a chair while an out of focus blonde girl in the background stood on her toes to take off a branch of autumn leaves from the frame of a mirror over a washbasin.<br/>To be honest with you, the photo was nothing special. Perhaps my friend was ironic. Or maybe not.<br/><br/>What I know is that from that day</span>
  <span id="freeText10575755042692948666" style="display:none"><br />As a non native English speaker, I discovered the adjective 'poignant' only six years ago thanks to a Canadian friend (thanks, Vicky). She chose it to comment a photo I took involving a bowler hat hanging from a chair while an out of focus blonde girl in the background stood on her toes to take off a branch of autumn leaves from the frame of a mirror over a washbasin.<br />To be honest with you, the photo was nothing special. Perhaps my friend was ironic. Or maybe not.<br /><br />What I know is that from that day on I have been struggling to find the right contest to use the same word.<br />The thing is that poignancy doesn't seem to apply to many things I see around me. Besides, the word 'poignant' doesn't come up very easily in conversation. 'Touching' and 'moving' are my natural choices.<br /><br />Now my quest is over. <br />For 'The Man Who Fell to Earth is a poignant novel'. I wouldn't call it in any other way. <br />It's certainly a sad story, but there is a delicate almost intimate feeling around it and within it that makes poignancy at home. I've never watched the movie adaptation taken from this book and starring David Bowie, but I am somewhat sceptical on the ability of the Hollywood industry to create and deliver the same atmosphere of the book. <br /><br />Walter Tevis was not your typical sci-fi writer and 'The Man Who Fell to Earth' is not your typical work of science fiction either. No surprise that Tevis himself referred at his so labelled 'sci-fi novels' (this one and Mockingbird are his most famous works) as 'speculative fiction' rather than science fiction.<br /><br />Given all that, you might not be surprised to find plenty of introspection here as well as recurring and symbolic references to paintings by Klee, Bruegel, Manet, and Van Gogh. Which is not the standard cup of tea for a sci-fi novelist. <br />At the same time, the 1980s and 1990s imagined by the author in 1963 are not that technologically advanced to leave you flabbergasted. No flying machines. No smell-o-vision cinemas. No androids dreaming of electring sheep. In fact, what happens is quite the opposite: there is no hint that humankind ever made it to the Moon (as it did only six years after this book got published) as well as that any significant leap forward took place either in mass production of goods or scientific research.<br /><br />This is not a coincidence. The author wants the readers to focus on the main character. <br />And the main character might look like a man, but - as it happens - doesn't belong to the human race but comes from planet Anthea. That he 'fell' to Earth and brought with himself enough blueprints and chemical formulas to give humankind progress and make himself a billionaire in the process is all a part of a masterplan. <br />Now the problem for the Anthean visitor is that he starts feeling overtired and lonely. If he were a man, he would soon discover that money can't buy health, love and happiness. But he comes from Anthea, accumulates cash for a purpose and doesn't seek for disillusion thus going straight into alcoholism.<br /><br />Walter Tevis was an expert on this. And I'm sure there's much of him in Mr Newton, the Anthean visitor. It's true that the author indulges way too much on what each character drinks, if they drink it straight, bitter or on the rocks and whether they stir their drink and,if so, for how long. <br />As a matter of fact, all the three main characters in the novel had, have or will have problem with the booze to the point that they often wish to get drunk. I cannot deny that this subplot is somehow simplistic: after all there's nothing new in alcohol seen as a painkiller, a nectar of wishful forgetting and - at the same time - a weapon of self destruction. <br /><br />And yet Tevis' writing made me forgive him for all that first hand insistence on alcohol. <br />What ultimately wins here is a powerful story that is beautifully told and is still topical today. The uneasiness of Mr Newton, the Man who Fell to Earth, first in dealing with Earthlings and then with himself is non extraterrestrial, but very much a human feeling. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10575755042692948666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41269098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41269098" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1302643-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1215689869p2/1302643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41269098?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/1302643-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who finds alcoholics fascinating</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1302643-brian?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41269098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17997887573291557941">The problem with this book is that author must have thought that the only way to make a person seem deep is to make them an alcoholic--alcoholism defines all three main characters in the book. It's like an actor who finally wins an academy award after playing a drunk (when it is probably the easiest part to play). The vast majority of interactions in the book include drinking, which Tevis describes in painstaking detail, over and over again--what they are drinking, how they are drinking it, what</span>
  <span id="freeText17997887573291557941" style="display:none">The problem with this book is that author must have thought that the only way to make a person seem deep is to make them an alcoholic--alcoholism defines all three main characters in the book. It's like an actor who finally wins an academy award after playing a drunk (when it is probably the easiest part to play). The vast majority of interactions in the book include drinking, which Tevis describes in painstaking detail, over and over again--what they are drinking, how they are drinking it, what the other characters think of what and how they are drinking, whether they stir their drinks or not, what the other characters think about whether they stirred or not. It's excessive. Actually, many of the non-interaction scenes are about the same. <br /><br />Personally, I don't find alcoholics all that interesting. However, the book itself still isn't bad. If the author had found more creative ways to express the tragic elements of the human experience (besides alcoholism), it could have been a great book. The story itself is intriguing and the essence of the book-- that this alien undergoes a very human experience--is novel and will stay with me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17997887573291557941" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2715961477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2715961477" />
    <a title="Willy Boy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90729162-willy-boy"><img alt="Willy Boy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555262190p2/90729162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2715961477?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willy Boy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willy Boy" href="/user/show/90729162-willy-boy">Willy Boy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2715961477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7915686225759549219">Great - if you love the movie, read it, it stands as a great book in it's own right, it has a life of it's own. If you didn't like the movie, the same applies. <br/><br/>It has (as the title suggests) an elegant parable-like simplicity, and contains an elegiac meditation on loss, of home, of potential, friendship, family ... Matters of conspiracy and corporate-politico hanky panky are touched upon. Placing an extra terrestrial in a contemporary setting lends the book a timeless quality (some with prejudic</span>
  <span id="freeText7915686225759549219" style="display:none">Great - if you love the movie, read it, it stands as a great book in it's own right, it has a life of it's own. If you didn't like the movie, the same applies. <br /><br />It has (as the title suggests) an elegant parable-like simplicity, and contains an elegiac meditation on loss, of home, of potential, friendship, family ... Matters of conspiracy and corporate-politico hanky panky are touched upon. Placing an extra terrestrial in a contemporary setting lends the book a timeless quality (some with prejudice against SF claim ambiguity over Mr Newton's alien origins - there isn't, in either film or book - there is no hardware or big 'event' UFO landing. Credibly low-key). A classic Rise &amp; Fall narrative, or One Long Fall. Before you can say JC, the hapless circumstances of a solitary advanced creature marooned in the lousey crudhole we sarcastically call civilisation is laid bare. Mankind, J'accuse! <br /><br />Far from depressing, thought provoking, a touch melancholic, moves at an exciting thriller-like pace, the poignant aspects aren't obvious until you finish. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7915686225759549219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JOur2zUk68Q1GX1eMXEmdRb3EVH/pdUEwQpnvLyN2eXH1hBR2Ebngk8ITfKjtBmPCEOaYVzw50itYR1IVWpT3w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('F3A7h8On2stptuxYVBoMljXW1hEGY0tynUKPkgEVfEb0TYANLsXWjROn3PTG3zNsK2JdIaU2eT7xKfVm6PL2fA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4448408.Walter_Tevis">About Walter Tevis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4448408.Walter_Tevis"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1296773197p3/4448408.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4448408.Walter_Tevis">Walter Tevis
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
383
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12807591879259063867">Walter Stone Tevis was an American novelist and short story writer. Three of his six novels were adapted into major films: The Hustler, The Color of Money and The Man Who Fell to Earth. The Queen's Gambit has also been adapted in 2020 into a 7-episode mini-series. His books have been translated into at least 18 languages.</span>

</div>
</div>
